City</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia"><img alt="Indonesia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 1.5x, style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia"><img alt="Malaysia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2800" Lumpur</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines"><img alt="Philippines" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="450" title="Manila">Manila</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia"><img alt="Indonesia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Medan" title="Medan">Medan</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore"><img alt="Singapore" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/23px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/35px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/45px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia"><img alt="Indonesia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines"><img alt="Philippines" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="900" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Flag_of_the_Philippines.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Philippines.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Flag_of_the_Philippines.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_Philippines.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Quezon_City" title="Quezon City">Quezon City</a>\n</li><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"> <span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar"><img alt="Myanmar" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Flag_of_Myanmar.svg/23px-Flag_of_Myanmar.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Flag_of_Myanmar.svg/35px-Flag_of_Myanmar.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Flag_of_Myanmar.svg/45px-Flag_of_Myanmar.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Yangon</a>\n</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/UN_M.49" title="UN M.49">UN M.49 code</a></th><td><code>035</code> &#8211; South-eastern Asia<br/><code>142</code> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a><br/><code>001</code> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">World</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Southeast Asia</b> or <b>Southeastern Asia</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Subregion" title="Subregion">subregion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, consisting of the countries that are geographically south of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, east of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, west of <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>, and north of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Southeast Asia is bordered to the north by <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, to the west by <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, to the east by <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, and to the south by <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>. The region is the only part of Asia that lies partly within the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, although the majority of it is in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. In contemporary definition, Southeast Asia consists of two geographic regions:\n</p>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a>, also known historically as <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>, comprising parts of <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Eastern India</a> (India stretches from South Asia to Southeast Asia), <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Malaysia" title="Peninsular Malaysia">West Malaysia</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a>, also known historically as <a href="/wiki/Nusantara" title="Nusantara">Nusantara</a>, the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a>, comprises the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Malaysia" title="East Malaysia">East Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Island" title="Christmas Island">Christmas Island</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cocos_(Keeling)_Islands" title="Cocos (Keeling) Islands">Cocos (Keeling) Islands</a>. <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> is also included in this grouping by many anthropologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>The region lies near the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">geological plates</a>, with both heavy seismic and volcanic activities. The <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Plate" title="Sunda Plate">Sunda Plate</a> is the main plate of the region, featuring almost all Southeast Asian countries except Myanmar, northern Thailand, northern Vietnam, and northern <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a> of the Philippines. The mountain ranges in Myanmar, Thailand, and peninsular Malaysia are part of the <a href="/wiki/Alpide_belt" title="Alpide belt">Alpide belt</a>, while the islands of the Philippines are part of the Pacific <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Fire" title="Ring of Fire">Ring of Fire</a>. Both seismic belts meet in Indonesia, causing the region to have relatively high occurrences of earthquakes and volcanic eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Southeast Asia covers about 4.5 million km<sup>2</sup> (1.7 million mi<sup>2</sup>), which is 10.5% of Asia or 3% of earth\'s total land area. Its total population is more than 641 million, about 8.5% of the world\'s population. It is the third most populous geographical region in the world after South Asia and East Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The region is culturally and ethnically diverse, with hundreds of languages spoken by different ethnic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Ten countries in the region are members of <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">ASEAN</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Regional_organization" title="Regional organization">regional organization</a> established for economic, political, military, educational and cultural integration amongst its members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Environment"><span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Ethnic_groups"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ethnic groups</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a tocsection-31"><a href="#Writing"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Writing</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The region, together with part of South Asia, was well known by Europeans as the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> or simply the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">Indies</a> until the 20th century. Chinese sources referred the region as <i>&#21335;&#27915;</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Nanyang_(region)" title="Nanyang (region)">Nanyang</a></i>), which literally means the "Southern Ocean." The mainland section of Southeast Asia was referred to as <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> by European geographers due to its location between China  and the Indian subcontinent and its having cultural influences from both neighboring regions. In the 20th century, however, the term became more restricted to territories of the former <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> (Cambodia, Laos, and Vietnam). The maritime section of Southeast Asia is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a>, a term derived from the European concept of a <a href="/wiki/Malay_race" title="Malay race">Malay race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace-10">[10]</a></sup> Another term for Maritime Southeast Asia is <i><a href="/wiki/Insulindia" title="Insulindia">Insulindia</a></i> (Indian Islands), used to describe the region between Indochina and <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term "Southeast Asia" was first used in 1839 by American pastor Howard Malcolm in his book <i>Travels in South-Eastern Asia</i>. Malcolm only included the Mainland section and excluded the Maritime section in his definition of Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The term was officially used in the midst of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a>, through the formation of <a href="/wiki/South_East_Asia_Command" title="South East Asia Command">South East Asia Command</a> (SEAC) in 1943.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> SEAC popularised the use of the term "Southeast Asia," although what constituted Southeast Asia was not fixed; for example, SEAC excluded the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and a large part of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> while including <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Ceylon</a>. However, by the late 1970s, a roughly standard usage of the term "Southeast Asia" and the territories it encompasses had emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerson_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-emmerson-14">[14]</a></sup> Although from a cultural or linguistic perspective the definitions of "Southeast Asia" may vary, the most common definitions nowadays include the area represented by the countries (sovereign states and dependent territories) listed below.\n</p><p>Ten of the eleven states of Southeast Asia are members of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">Association of Southeast Asian Nations</a> (ASEAN), while <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a> is an observer state. <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> has stated that it might join ASEAN, and is currently an observer. Sovereignty issues exist over some <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea_Islands" title="South China Sea Islands">territories in the South China Sea</a>.\n</p><p>Some southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">Mainland China</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> (a disputed region or nation), are also considered as part of Southeast Asia by some authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_divisions">Political divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Political divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sovereign_states">Sovereign states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sovereign states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<table class="sortable wikitable" style="text-align:right;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th scope="col">State\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Area<br/>(km<sup>2</sup>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-IMF-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Population<br/>(2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th scope="col">Density<br/>(/km<sup>2</sup>)\n</th>\n<th scope="col">GDP (nominal),<br/>USD (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMF_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-IMF-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographical divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Southeast Asia is geographically divided into two subregions, namely <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a> (or the similarly defined <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">Malay Archipelago</a>) (<a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">Javanese</a>: <span lang="jv">Nusantara</span>).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a> includes:\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myanmar_(Burma)" title="Myanmar (Burma)">Myanmar (Burma)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Malaysia" title="Peninsular Malaysia">Peninsular Malaysia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a> includes:\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/East_Malaysia" title="East Malaysia">East Malaysia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> of India are geographically considered part of Maritime Southeast Asia. Eastern <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a> have strong cultural ties with Southeast Asia and sometimes considered both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asian" title="South Asian">South Asian</a> and Southeast Asian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> has on some occasions been considered a part of Southeast Asia because of its cultural ties to mainland Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emmerson_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-emmerson-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">rest</a> of the island of <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> which is not part of Indonesia, namely, Papua New Guinea, is sometimes included, and so are <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mariana_Islands" title="Northern Mariana Islands">Northern Mariana Islands</a>, which were all part of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_East_Indies" title="Spanish East Indies">Spanish East Indies</a> with strong cultural and linguistic ties to the region, specifically, the Philippines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The eastern half of Indonesia and East Timor (east of the <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Line" title="Wallace Line">Wallace Line</a>) are considered to be biogeographically part of Oceania (<a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a>) due to its distinctive faunal features. <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> and its surrounding islands are geologically considered as a part of <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australian continent</a>, connected via the <a href="/wiki/Sahul_Shelf" title="Sahul Shelf">Sahul Shelf</a>.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div style="position:absolute;top:49.904%;left:20.834%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand"><img alt="Gulf of Thailand" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Turquoise_pog.svg/8px-Turquoise_pog.svg.png" Thailand</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:26.431%;left:29.771%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tonkin" title="Gulf of Tonkin</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:35.401%;left:3.739%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal"><img alt="Bay of Bengal" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Blue_pog.svg/8px-Blue_pog.svg.png" class="nowrap">Bay of Bengal</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:85.4%;left:3.739%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-5px;top:-5px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" Ocean</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:62.499%;left:17.726%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:76.24%;left:45.701%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Makassar_Strait" title="Makassar Tomini</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:85.217%;left:26.865%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait"><img alt="Sunda Strait" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="100" decoding="async" height="8" Strait</span></div></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:55.973%;left:53.472%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><a href="/wiki/Moro_Gulf" title="Moro Gulf"><img alt="Moro Gulf" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Turquoise_pog.svg/8px-Turquoise_pog.svg.png" style="font-size:90%;line-height:110%;position:absolute;width:6em;top:-0.75em;left:5px;text-align:left"><div style="display:inline;padding:1px;float:left"><span class="nowrap">Moro Gulf</span></div></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southeast_Asia_location_map.svg" title="File:Southeast Asia location map.svg"> </a></div><div style="text-align:center;font-weight:bold;">Oceans and Seas in Southeast Asia</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Southeast_Asia" title="History of Southeast Asia">History of Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="523" decoding="async" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Megalitisch_beeld_bij_Tegoerwangi_TMnr_10025807.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A megalithic statue found in Tegurwangi, <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>. 1500 CE</div></div></div>\n<p>The region was already inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a> from 1,000,000 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a> age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[25]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> reached the region by around 45,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> having moved eastwards from the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">Rock art</a> (parietal art) dating from 40,000 years ago (which is currently the world\'s oldest) has been discovered in the caves of <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></i> also lived in the area up until 12,000 years ago, when they became extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> It has been proposed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_people" title="Austronesian people">Austronesian people</a>, who form the majority of the modern population in Indonesia, Malaysia, Brunei, East Timor, and the Philippines, may have migrated to Southeast Asia from <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. They arrived in Indonesia around 2000&#160;BC, and as they spread through the archipelago, they often settled along coastal areas and confined indigenous peoples such as <a href="/wiki/Orang_Asli" title="Orang Asli">Orang Asli</a> of peninsular Malaysia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negritos" title="Negritos">Negritos</a> of the Philippines or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papuans" title="Papuans">Papuans</a> of New Guinea to inland regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Archaeologists refer these people as <i>Deutero-Malays</i>, whom are more advanced in farming techniques and metal knowledge than their indigenous counterpart, the <i>Proto-Malays</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies presented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_Genome_Organization" title="Human Genome Organization">HUGO (Human Genome Organization)</a> through genetic studies of the various peoples of Asia, show empirically that there was a single migration event from Africa, whereby the early people travelled along the south coast of Asia, first entered the Malay peninsula 50,000&#8211;90,000 years ago. The Orang Asli, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Semang" title="Semang">Semang</a> who show Negrito characteristics, are the direct descendants of these earliest settlers of Southeast Asia. These early people diversified and travelled slowly northwards to China, and the populations of Southeast Asia show greater genetic diversity than the younger population of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">[32]</a></sup> Studies on the genetics of modern Malays however show that there is a complex history of admixture of human populations in Southeast Asia, with the Malay population showing four major ancestral components: Austronesian, Proto-Malay, East Asian, and South Asian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Solheim and others have shown evidence for a <i>Nusantao</i> (<i>Nusantara</i>) maritime trading network ranging from <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> to the rest of the archipelago as early as 5000 BC to 1 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Dong_Son_culture" title="Dong Son culture">Dong Son culture</a> flourished in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Vietnam" title="Northern Vietnam">Northern Vietnam</a> from about 1000 BC to 1 BC. Its influence spread to other parts Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The region entered the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> era in 500 BC, when iron was forged also in northern Vietnam still under Dong Son, due to its frequent interactions with neighboring China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The peoples of Southeast Asia, especially those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian" title="Austronesian">Austronesian</a> descent, have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seafarers" title="Seafarers">seafarers</a> for thousands of years, some reaching the island of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, became the ancestors of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people">Malagasy people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-39">[39]</a></sup> Passage through the Indian Ocean aided the colonisation of Madagascar, as well as commerce between Western Asia, eastern coast of India and Chinese southern coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-:22-39">[39]</a></sup> Gold from <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> is thought to have reached as far west as Rome. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> wrote in his <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a> about <a href="/wiki/Chryse_and_Argyre" title="Chryse and Argyre">Chryse and Argyre</a>, two legendary islands rich in gold and silver, located in the Indian Ocean. Their vessels, such as the <a href="/wiki/Vinta" title="Vinta">vinta</a>, were capable to sail across ocean. <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Magellan\'s</a> voyage records how much more manoeuvrable their vessels were, as compared to the European ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen.L_Magellan_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen.L_Magellan-40">[40]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Enrique_of_Malacca" title="Enrique of Malacca">slave</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sulu_Sea" title="Sulu Sea">Sulu Sea</a> was believed to have been used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magellan%27s_voyage" title="Magellan\'s voyage">Magellan\'s voyage</a> as a translator.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vietnam,_tamburo_di_song_da,_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9,_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II,_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1830" data-file-width="1906" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG/220px-Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG/330px-Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG/440px-Vietnam%2C_tamburo_di_song_da%2C_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9%2C_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II%2C_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vietnam,_tamburo_di_song_da,_detto_tamburo_Mouli%C3%A9,_cultura_di_S%C3%B4ng_D%C3%B4ng_Son_II,_I_millennio_ac._01.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze drum from <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B4ng_%C4%90%C3%A0_(M%C6%B0%E1%BB%9Dng_Lay)" title="S&#244;ng &#272;&#224; (M&#432;&#7901;ng Lay)">S&#244;ng &#272;&#224;</a>, northern Vietnam. Mid-1st millennium BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Most Southeast Asian people were originally <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animist</a>, engaged in ancestors, nature, and spirits worship. These belief systems were later supplanted by <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and Buddhism after the region, especially coastal areas, came under contacts with <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> during the 1st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jgonda_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-jgonda-41">[41]</a></sup> Indian Brahmins and traders brought Hinduism to the region and made contacts with local courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Local rulers converted to Hinduism or Buddhism and adopted Indian religious traditions to reinforce their legitimacy, elevate ritual status above their fellow chief counterparts and facilitate trade with South Asian states. They periodically invited Indian Brahmins into their realms and began a gradual process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indianized_kingdom" title="Indianized kingdom">Indianisation</a> in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanaik_1997_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanaik_1997-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery_2002_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery_2002-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a> was the dominant religious tradition of many southern Indian Hindu kingdoms during the 1st century. It then spread into Southeast Asia via <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, Indochina, then Malay Archipelago, leading to thousands of Shiva temples on the islands of Indonesia as well as Cambodia and Vietnam, co-evolving with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bisschopoup2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-bisschopoup2-47">[47]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a> entered the region during the 3rd century, via maritime trade routes between the region and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Buddhism later established a strong presence in <a href="/wiki/Funan" title="Funan">Funan</a> region in the 5th century. In present-day mainland Southeast Asia, Theravada is still the dominant branch of Buddhism, practiced by the Thai, Burmese and Cambodian Buddhists. This branch was fused with the Hindu-influenced Khmer culture. <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana Buddhism</a> established presence in Maritime Southeast Asia, brought by Chinese monks during their transit in the region en route to <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-43">[43]</a></sup> It is still the dominant branch of Buddhism practiced by Indonesian and Malaysian Buddhists.\n</p><p>The spread of these two Indian religions confined the adherents of Southeast Asian indigenous beliefs into remote inland areas. <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku Islands</a> and New Guinea were never been Indianised and its native people were predominantly animists until the 15th century when <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> began to spread in those areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> While in Vietnam, Buddhism never managed to develop strong institutional networks due to strong Chinese influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In present-day Southeast Asia, Vietnam is the only country where <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_folk_religion" title="Vietnamese folk religion">its folk religion</a> makes up the plurality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Recently, Vietnamese folk religion is undergoing a revival with the support of the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Elsewhere, there are ethnic groups in Southeast Asia that resist conversion and still retain their original animist beliefs, such as the <a href="/wiki/Dayak_people" title="Dayak people">Dayaks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kalimantan" title="Kalimantan">Kalimantan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Igorot_people" title="Igorot people">Igorots</a> in Luzon, and the <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shans</a> in eastern Myanmar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu_and_Buddhist_kingdoms_era">Hindu and Buddhist kingdoms era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hindu and Buddhist kingdoms era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greater_India" title="Greater India">Greater India</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Indian_influence_on_Southeast_Asia" title="History of Indian influence on Southeast Asia">History of Indian influence on Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1108" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg/220px-Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg/330px-Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg/440px-Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur-Nothwest-view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After the region came under contacts with Indian subcontinent circa 400 BCE, it began a gradual process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indianized_kingdom" title="Indianized kingdom">Indianisation</a> where Indian ideas such as religions, cultures, architectures and political administrations were brought by traders and religious figures and adopted by local rulers. In turn, Indian Brahmins and monks were invited by local rulers to live in their realms and help transforming local polities to become more Indianised, blending Indian and indigenous traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanaik_1997_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vanaik_1997-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery_2002_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery_2002-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a> became the elite language of the region, which effectively made Southeast Asia part of the <a href="/wiki/Indosphere" title="Indosphere">Indosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Most of the region had been Indianised during the first centuries, while the Philippines later Indianised circa 9th century when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tondo" title="Kingdom of Tondo">Kingdom of Tondo</a> was established in Luzon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Postma_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Postma-57">[57]</a></sup> Vietnam, especially its northern part, was never fully Indianised due to the many periods of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_domination_of_Vietnam" title="Chinese domination of Vietnam">Chinese domination</a> it experienced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first Indian-influenced polities established in the region were the <a href="/wiki/Pyu_city-states" title="Pyu city-states">Pyu city-states</a> that already existed circa 2nd century BCE, located in inland Myanmar. It served as an overland trading hub between India and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Theravada Buddhism was the predominant religion of these city states, while the presence of other Indian religions such as Mahayana Buddhism and Hinduism were also widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mat-31-34_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mat-31-34-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mha-15-17_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-mha-15-17-61">[61]</a></sup> In the 1st century, the <a href="/wiki/Funan" title="Funan">Funan</a> states centered in <a href="/wiki/Mekong_Delta" title="Mekong Delta">Mekong Delta</a> were established, encompassed modern-day Cambodia, southern Vietnam, Laos, and eastern Thailand. It became the dominant trading power in mainland Southeast Asia for about five centuries, provided passage for Indian and Chinese goods and assumed authority over the flow of commerce through Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-:22-39">[39]</a></sup> In maritime Southeast Asia, the first recorded Indianised kingdom was <a href="/wiki/Salakanagara" title="Salakanagara">Salakanagara</a>, established in western Java circa 2nd century CE. This Hindu kingdom was known by the Greeks as <i>Argyre</i> (Land of Silver).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg/220px-Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg/330px-Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg/440px-Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hinduism_Expansion_in_Asia.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spread of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_culture" title="Indian culture">Indian culture</a> in Southeast Asia</div></div></div>\n<p>By the 5th century CE, trade networking between East and West was concentrated in the maritime route. Foreign traders were starting to use new routes such as <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Malacca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">Sunda Strait</a> due to the development of maritime Southeast Asia. This change resulted in the decline of Funan, while new maritime powers such as <a href="/wiki/Srivijaya" title="Srivijaya">Srivijaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarumanagara" title="Tarumanagara">Tarumanagara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medang_Kingdom" title="Medang Kingdom">Medang</a> emerged. Srivijaya especially became the dominant maritime power for more than 5 centuries, controlling both <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">Sunda Strait</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-:22-39">[39]</a></sup> This dominance started to decline when Srivijaya were <a href="/wiki/Chola_invasion_of_Srivijaya" title="Chola invasion of Srivijaya">invaded by Chola Empire</a>, a dominant maritime power of Indian subcontinent, in 1025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-C._Majumdar_1961_pp._338-342-63">[63]</a></sup> The invasion reshaped power and trade in the region, resulted in the rise of new regional powers such as the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Empire" title="Khmer Empire">Khmer Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kahuripan" title="Kahuripan">Kahuripan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Continued commercial contacts with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Imperial_China" title="Mid-Imperial China">Chinese Empire</a> enabled the Cholas to influence the local cultures. Many of the surviving examples of the <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Southeast_Asia" title="Hinduism in Southeast Asia">Hindu cultural influence</a> found today throughout Southeast Asia are the result of the Chola expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prambanan_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-prambanan-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siem_Reap_Reflections_(CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT)_VI_(1070423631).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2572" data-file-width="3960" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg/220px-Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg/330px-Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg/440px-Siem_Reap_Reflections_%28CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT%29_VI_%281070423631%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siem_Reap_Reflections_(CAMBODIA-REFLECTION-ANGKOR_WAT)_VI_(1070423631).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a> in <a href="/wiki/Siem_Reap" title="Siem Reap">Siem Reap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>As Srivijaya influence in the region declined, The Hindu Khmer Empire experienced a golden age during the 11th to 13th century CE. The empire\'s capital <a href="/wiki/Angkor" title="Angkor">Angkor</a> hosts majestic monuments&#8212;such as <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bayon" title="Bayon">Bayon</a>. Satellite imaging has revealed that Angkor, during its peak, was the largest pre-industrial urban centre in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans2007_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans2007-66">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a> civilisation was located in what is today central Vietnam, and was a highly Indianised Hindu Kingdom. The <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_people" title="Vietnamese people">Vietnamese</a> launched a massive conquest against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cham_(Asia)" title="Cham (Asia)">Cham people</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1471_Vietnamese_invasion_of_Champa" title="1471 Vietnamese invasion of Champa">1471 Vietnamese invasion of Champa</a>, ransacking and burning Champa, slaughtering thousands of Cham people, and forcibly assimilating them into Vietnamese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 13th century CE, the region experienced <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol invasions</a>, affected areas such as Vietnamese coast, inland Burma and <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>. In 1258, 1285 and 1287, the Mongols tried to invade <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%E1%BA%A1i_Vi%E1%BB%87t" title="&#272;&#7841;i Vi&#7879;t">&#272;&#7841;i Vi&#7879;t</a> and <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The invasions were unsuccessful, yet both Dai Viet and Champa agreed to become tributary states to <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> to avoid further conflicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The Mongols also invaded <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a> in Burma from 1277 to 1287, resulted in fragmentation of the Kingdom and rise of smaller <a href="/wiki/Shan_States" title="Shan States">Shan States</a> ruled by local chieftains nominally submitted to Yuan dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> However, in 1297, a new local power emerged. <a href="/wiki/Myinsaing_Kingdom" title="Myinsaing Kingdom">Myinsaing Kingdom</a> became the real ruler of Central Burma and challenged the Mongol rule. This resulted in the second Mongol invasion of Burma in 1300, which was repulsed by Myinsaing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The Mongols would later in 1303 withdrawn from Burma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1292, The Mongols sent envoys to <a href="/wiki/Singhasari" title="Singhasari">Singhasari</a> Kingdom in Java to ask for submission to Mongol rule. Singhasari rejected the proposal and injured the envoys, enraged the Mongols and made them sent a large invasion fleet to Java. Unbeknownst to them, Singhasari collapsed in 1293 due to a revolt by Kadiri, one of its vassals. When the Mongols arrived in Java, a local prince named <a href="/wiki/Raden_Wijaya" title="Raden Wijaya">Raden Wijaya</a> offered his service to assist the Mongols in punishing Kadiri. After Kadiri was defeated, Wijaya turned on his Mongol allies, ambushed their invasion fleet and forced them to immediately leave Java.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the departure of the Mongols, Wijaya established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majapahit_Empire" title="Majapahit Empire">Majapahit Empire</a> in eastern Java in 1293. Majapahit would soon grew into a regional power. Its greatest ruler was <a href="/wiki/Hayam_Wuruk" title="Hayam Wuruk">Hayam Wuruk</a>, whose reign from 1350 to 1389 marked the empire\'s peak when other kingdoms in the southern <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a> came under its influence. Various sources such as the Nagarakertagama also mention that its influence spanned over parts of <a href="/wiki/Sulawesi" title="Sulawesi">Sulawesi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku</a>, and some areas of <a href="/wiki/Western_New_Guinea" title="Western New Guinea">western New Guinea</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, making it one of the largest empire to ever exist in Southeast Asian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miksic2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-miksic2-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">(<span>p107</span>)</sup> By the 15th century CE however, Majapahit\'s influence began to wane due to many war of successions it experienced and the rise of new Islamic states such as <a href="/wiki/Samudera_Pasai_Sultanate" title="Samudera Pasai Sultanate">Samudera Pasai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malacca_Sultanate" title="Malacca Sultanate">Malacca Sultanate</a> around the strategic <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a>. Majapahit then collapsed around 1500. It was the last major Hindu kingdom and the last regional power in the region before the arrival of the Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_Islam">Spread of Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Spread of Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam_in_Southeast_Asia" title="Spread of Islam in Southeast Asia">Spread of Islam in Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Southeast_Asia" title="Islam in Southeast Asia">Islam in Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Around-Kota-Bharu-(19).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1128" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg/220px-Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg/330px-Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg/440px-Around-Kota-Bharu-%2819%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Around-Kota-Bharu-(19).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kampung_Laut_Mosque" title="Kampung Laut Mosque">Kampung Laut Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tumpat_District" title="Tumpat District">Tumpat</a> is one of the oldest mosques in Malaysia, dating to the early 18th century.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> began to made contacts with Southeast Asia in the 8th-century CE, when the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyads</a> established trade with the region via sea routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> However its spread into the region happened centuries later. In the 11th century, a turbulent period occurred in the history of <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chola" title="Chola">Chola</a> navy crossed the ocean and attacked the <a href="/wiki/Srivijaya" title="Srivijaya">Srivijaya</a> kingdom of Sangrama Vijayatungavarman in Kadaram (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kedah_Kingdom" title="Kedah Kingdom">Kedah</a>); the capital of the powerful maritime kingdom was sacked and the king was taken captive. Along with Kadaram, Pannai in present-day <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> and Malaiyur and the Malayan peninsula were attacked too. Soon after that, the king of Kedah Phra Ong Mahawangsa became the first ruler to abandon the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> faith, and converted to Islam with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Kedah" title="Sultanate of Kedah">Sultanate of Kedah</a> established in 1136. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samudera_Pasai" title="Samudera Pasai">Samudera Pasai</a> converted to Islam in 1267, the King of Malacca <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parameswara_(sultan)" title="Parameswara (sultan)">Parameswara</a> married the princess of Pasai, and the son became the first sultan of Malacca. Soon, Malacca became the center of Islamic study and maritime trade, and other rulers followed suit. <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> religious leader and Islamic scholar <a href="/wiki/Hamka" title="Hamka">Hamka</a> (1908&#8211;1981) wrote in 1961: "The development of Islam in Indonesia and <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Malaya" title="Federation of Malaya">Malaya</a> is intimately related to a Chinese Muslim, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_Koranschool_op_Java_TMnr_10002385.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children studying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur\'an">Qur\'an</a> in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, Indonesia, during <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">colonial period</a></div></div></div>\n<p>There are several theories to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamisation" title="Islamisation">Islamisation</a> process in Southeast Asia. Another theory is trade. The expansion of trade among West Asia, India and Southeast Asia helped the spread of the religion as Muslim traders from Southern Yemen (Hadramout) brought Islam to the region with their large volume of trade. Many settled in Indonesia, Singapore, and Malaysia. This is evident in the Arab-Indonesian, Arab-Singaporean, and Arab-Malay populations who were at one time very prominent in each of their countries. Finally, the ruling classes embraced Islam and that further aided the permeation of the religion throughout the region. The ruler of the region\'s most important port, <a href="/wiki/Malacca_Sultanate" title="Malacca Sultanate">Malacca Sultanate</a>, embraced Islam in the 15th century, heralding a period of accelerated conversion of Islam throughout the region as Islam provided a positive force among the ruling and trading classes. <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_Muslims" title="Gujarati Muslims">Gujarati Muslims</a> played a pivotal role in establishing <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toi_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-toi-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_foreign_colonisation">Trade and foreign colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Trade and foreign colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StraitOfMalacca2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="695" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/StraitOfMalacca2.jpg/220px-StraitOfMalacca2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/StraitOfMalacca2.jpg/330px-StraitOfMalacca2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/88/StraitOfMalacca2.jpg/440px-StraitOfMalacca2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StraitOfMalacca2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Trade among Southeast Asian countries has a long tradition. The consequences of colonial rule, struggle for independence and in some cases war influenced the economic attitudes and policies of each country until today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">List of tributaries of Imperial China</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Empire" title="Chinese Empire">Chinese Empire</a></div>\n<p>From 111&#160;BC to 938 AD northern Vietnam was under Chinese rule. Vietnam was successfully governed by a series of Chinese dynasties including the Han, Eastern Han, Eastern Wu, Cao Wei, Jin, Liu Song, Southern Qi, Liang, Sui, Tang, and Southern Han.\n</p><p>Records from Magellan\'s voyage show that <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a> possessed more <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a> than European ships, so the Chinese must have been trading with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen.L_Magellan_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen.L_Magellan-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Malaysian legend has it that a Chinese Ming emperor sent a princess, <a href="/wiki/Hang_Li_Po" title="Hang Li Po">Hang Li Po</a>, to Malacca, with a retinue of 500, to marry <a href="/wiki/Mansur_Shah_of_Malacca" title="Mansur Shah of Malacca">Sultan Mansur Shah</a> after the emperor was impressed by the wisdom of the sultan. Han Li Po\'s well (constructed 1459) is now a tourist attraction there, as is <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Cina" title="Bukit Cina">Bukit Cina</a>, where her retinue settled.\n</p><p>The strategic value of the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a>, which was controlled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Malacca" title="Sultanate of Malacca">Sultanate of Malacca</a> in the 15th and early 16th century, did not go unnoticed by <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a>, who in 1500 wrote "He who is lord of Malacca has his hand on the throat of <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>".\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="European">European</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: European">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG/220px-Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG/330px-Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG/440px-Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Cornwallis_Eck.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fort_Cornwallis" title="Fort Cornwallis">Fort Cornwallis</a> in <a href="/wiki/George_Town,_Penang" title="George Town, Penang">George Town</a> marks the spot where the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">British East India Company</a> first landed in <a href="/wiki/Penang" title="Penang">Penang</a> in 1786, thus heralding the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British colonisation</a> of <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/European_colonisation_of_Southeast_Asia" title="European colonisation of Southeast Asia">European colonisation of Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<p>Western influence started to enter in the 16th century, with the arrival of the Portuguese in Malacca, <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku</a> and the Philippines, the latter being settled by the Spanish years later. Throughout the 17th and 18th centuries the Dutch established the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a>; the French <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>; and the British <a href="/wiki/Straits_Settlements" title="Straits Settlements">Strait Settlements</a>. By the 19th century, all Southeast Asian countries were colonised except for <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kopparmynt,_1646-1667._2_kas,_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2445" data-file-width="2445" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Kopparmynt%2C_1646-1667._2_kas%2C_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kopparmynt,_1646-1667._2_kas,_2_doit._Holl%C3%A4ndska_Indien_-_Skoklosters_slott_-_108653.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Duit" title="Duit">Duit</a>, a coin minted by the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">VOC</a>, 1646&#8211;1667. 2 kas, 2 duit.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_European_exploration_of_Asia" title="Chronology of European exploration of Asia">European explorers</a> were reaching Southeast Asia from the west and from the east. Regular trade between the ships sailing east from the Indian Ocean and south from mainland Asia provided goods in return for natural products, such as <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hornbill" title="Hornbill">hornbill</a> beaks from the islands of the archipelago.\nBefore the eighteenth and nineteenth century, the Europeans mostly were interested in expanding trade links. For the majority of the populations in each country, there was comparatively little interaction with Europeans and traditional social routines and relationships continued. For most, a life with subsistence level agriculture, fishing and, in less developed civilizations, hunting and gathering was still hard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Europeans brought <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> allowing Christian missionaries to become widespread. Thailand also allowed Western scientists to enter its country to develop its own education system as well as start sending Royal members and Thai scholars to get higher education from Europe and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greater_East_Asia_Co-Prosperity_Sphere" title="Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere">Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes" title="Japanese war crimes">Japanese war crimes</a></div>\n<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Imperial Japan</a> invaded most of the former western colonies. The <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_period" title="Sh&#333;wa period">Sh&#333;wa occupation regime</a> committed violent actions against civilians such as the <a href="/wiki/Manila_massacre" title="Manila massacre">Manila massacre</a> and the implementation of a system of <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">forced labour</a>, such as the one involving 4 to 10 million <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romusha" title="Romusha">romusha</a></i> in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> A later UN report stated that four million people died in Indonesia as a result of famine and forced labour during the Japanese occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The Allied powers who defeated Japan in the <a href="/wiki/South-East_Asian_theatre_of_World_War_II" title="South-East Asian theatre of World War II">South-East Asian theatre of World War II</a> then contended with nationalists to whom the occupation authorities had granted independence.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian">Indian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Indian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> had a flourishing trade relationship with Southeast Asia in the 15th and 16th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toi_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-toi-84">[84]</a></sup> The trade relationship with Gujarat declined after the Portuguese invasion of Southeast Asia in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toi_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-toi-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_history">Contemporary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Contemporary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_foreign_policy_on_Southeast_Asia" title="Japanese foreign policy on Southeast Asia">Japanese foreign policy on Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<p>Most countries in the region enjoy national autonomy. <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">Democratic forms of government</a> and the recognition of human rights are taking root. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">ASEAN</a> provides a framework for the integration of commerce, and regional responses to international concerns.\n</p><p>China has asserted broad claims over the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, based on its <a href="/wiki/Nine-Dash_Line" title="Nine-Dash Line">Nine-Dash Line</a>, and has <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_Sand" title="Great Wall of Sand">built artificial islands</a> in an attempt to bolster its claims. China also has asserted an <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zone</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Spratly_Islands" title="Spratly Islands">Spratly Islands</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> challenged China in the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_Arbitration" title="Permanent Court of Arbitration">Permanent Court of Arbitration</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> in 2013, and in <i><a href="/wiki/Philippines_v._China" title="Philippines v. China">Philippines v. China</a></i> (2016), the Court ruled in favor of the Philippines and rejected China\'s claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeast_Asia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Triangle_(Southeast_Asia)" title="Golden Triangle (Southeast Asia)">Golden Triangle (Southeast Asia)</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Southeast_Asian_mountains" title="List of Southeast Asian mountains">List of Southeast Asian mountains</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zomia_(geography)" title="Zomia (geography)">Zomia (geography)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Southeast_asia.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="506" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Southeast_asia.svg/220px-Southeast_asia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Southeast_asia.svg/330px-Southeast_asia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Southeast_asia.svg/440px-Southeast_asia.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Southeast_asia.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relief map of Southeast Asia.</div></div></div>\n<p>Indonesia is the largest country in Southeast Asia and it also the largest archipelago in the world by size (according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>). Geologically. the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_archipelago" title="Indonesian archipelago">Indonesian archipelago</a> is one of the most <a href="/wiki/Volcanology" title="Volcanology">volcanically</a> active regions in the world. Geological <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">uplifts</a> in the region have also produced some impressive mountains, culminating in <a href="/wiki/Puncak_Jaya" title="Puncak Jaya">Puncak Jaya</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papua_(Indonesian_province)" title="Papua (Indonesian province)">Papua</a>, Indonesia at 5,030 metres (16,500 feet), on the island of <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>; it is the only place where ice glaciers can be found in Southeast Asia. The highest mountain in Southeast Asia is Hkakabo Razi at 5,967 meters and can be found in northern Burma sharing the same range of its parent peak, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> is the major body of water within Southeast Asia.
